		<date>1999-10-07</date>
		<username>Joey Tayler (joeyct@prodigy.net)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The best action movie since "Face/Off," and one of the year's best period</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Three Kings" is a film whose wit, ingenuity, and hyperactive momentum catch you of guard. This is a movie that, like so many great movies, flippantly refuses to conform to the rules we feel exist for particular genres, and that's a great deal of what makes watching it such a thrilling experience. It's hard enough to just make a great action movie when Hollywood views a good screenplay as subordinate to computer graphics and explosions, but "Three Kings" extends its ambitions beyond the simple label of "action movie." With an off-beat irreverence and surprisingly thoughtful and cynical political undercurrent, writer/director David O. Russell immediately grabs your attention and never lets go, but it's the level of humansim that elevates this wholly original and uniformly thrilling film to greatness.The Persian Gulf War has just come to an end, but four soldiers think they've found a way to benefit from the U.S.-Iraqi peace treaty. While rounding up Iraqi prisoners, Troy Barlow (Mark Wahlberg) and his best friend Conrad Vig (Spike Jonze) find a map hidden that is, shall we say, well hidden on one of the men they are searching. They bring it to Chief Elgin (Ice Cube), who determines that they've found a map pinpointing bunkers filled with loot Saddam stole from the Kuwaites. When story-hungry reporter Adriana Cruz (Nora Dunn) hears rumors that the bunkers on the map are storehouses for gold bullion, she imparts the information to her military escort, Archie Gates (George Clooney). Gates, disgruntled with his assignment and distraught over the pointlessness he sees in the army's involvement in the Persian Gulf, immediately ditches the reporter, finds the map and the soldiers holding it, steals a Humvee, and sets off into the desert with his new comrades.Gates thinks the gold should be theirs for the taking - the Iraqi soldiers are currently preoccupied with restoring order among rebellious citizens. But once the soldiers locate a hidden bunker, they're pulled into the political situation that is tearing apart life for the locals. "Bush told the people to rise up against Saddam," notes Gates, but now that the U.S. has protected its own interests the rebels are left with nothing to protect them from Saddam's retribution. Gates' troop watches Iraqi soldiers hit a milk truck with a rocket launcher, and moved by the starving people scooping milk out of puddles in the sand, they pass out their rations and water bottles. After witnessing a graphic execution, Gates intervenes to save a prisoner, and the shootout that accidently ensues (a brilliant scene that somehow manages to use slow-motion in a way that didn't make me think of John Woo) ties the fate of the soliders to the Iraqis. Troy is taken hostage, but resistance movement will help the Americans with the gold and in locating Troy if the soldiers will, in turn, escort the refugees to a camp just inside the Iranian border.Like many great movies, "Three Kings" finds a fundamental strength in the quality of its characters. There isn't a bad performance in the bunch, but George Clooney and Mark Wahlberg are the clear standouts. Finally we find Clooney with a character that fits well with his acting sensibilties; in Gates he finds a man who, much like the "ER" role that brought him stardom, has a considerable undercurrent of compassion and sensitivity coursing beneath his initially commanding, cynical surface. And Wahlberg only continues to show a deeper and deeper screen presence with each movie. Like Clooney, he's naturally commanding during action sequences, but we also see a lot of the touching naivity and boyishness that made him so effective in "Boogie Nights" as well. What Russell's screenplay does very effectively then is take its sharply defined characters and give them, and us, a bit of a culture shock - the shock being that Desert Storm might have made very little difference to some of the very people the U.S. claimed to be helping. In its own, more offhand way, "Three Kings" is just as insightful and articulate about the grey areas in American military action as "Saving Private Ryan" is. Where "Ryan" argued that war could be a necessary, but lamentable atrocity, "Three Kings" sees atrocity in the way that what America viewed as a necessity lead to a greater slaughter that America then did nothing to stop. There's a darkly funny scene late in the movie in which Clooney tries to con an enterprising Iraqi into giving him a fleet of new cars with the promise that the soldiers and the Iraqi rebels will unite and expel Saddam together. "George Bush wants you!" yells Clooney, and as he rallies the rebels into an uproar Russell is showing us the kind of unfulfilled promises the U.S. made in the name of protecting some oil interests.The compassion and understanding that Russell's screenplay holds for the Arab situation does not produce any kind of melodrama or simplistic portrayals of the Iraqis on either side of the conflict however. There's a scene in the middle of the film, in which Wahlberg's character talks to his Iraqi captor about his daughter and American reasons for being in the Persian Gulf that ranks among the great movie moments of the '90s. The Iraqi has lost both his wife and young son due to American bombing, and Wahlberg starts talking about his little girl back home "because we're both fathers." Both men also joined the army for no other reason than to make a little extra money by helping out their country. Each man understands the other's reasons for being in the situation they are both in. Each man recognizes similarities with the other, and that's part of what makes Russell's approach so thought-provoking. "Three Kings" isn't pro-American, and it isn't really pro-Iraqi either. Instead, the film equates the problems soldiers on both sides of the war are dealing with in terms of importance. Afterall, it wasn't Wahlberg's decision to bomb that man's home - everyone is at the whim of higher powers who are acting with questionable rational.Nothing I've written so far, however, properly conveys just what a viscerally exciting film "Three Kings" is; this movie has a rambunctious comedic spirit and a visual ingenuity all its own. But when all the bullets have been fired and when all the C-4-laced Nerf footballs have been blown up, it's the film's thoughtfulness on such a complex issue that gives "Three Kings" real staying power. It's the best action movie this country has produced since "Face/Off," and one of the year's best films period.</fullReview>
	</review>

Sergeant Major Archie Gates [George Clooney], who's about to retire, and Chief Elgin [Ice Cube], a devout Catholic. Then there's Sergeant Troy Barlow [Mark Whalberg], your average All-American, and Private Conrad Vig [Spike Jonze], Barlow's admiring pal. The four, after "unearthing" a map pointing out the way to bunkers where Saddam Hussien is storing gold bullion stolen from Kuwait, set off to make themselves a lifetime fortune, with the Beach Boys blasting on the radio. But they get more then they bargained for...excellent directing, dialouge, high-quality and solid acting. A modern-day masterpiece filled with blistering action and satire...a must-see.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-10-06</date>
		<username>allyjack</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A beautifully structured film of enormous confidence</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A beautifully structured film of enormous confidence, as assured in tragedy as in comedy, that rigorously pursues the apparent inanity of US policy - fighting under a banner of liberating Kuwait, yet unwilling to support the less economically loaded cause of the Iraqi rebels against Saddam: in the best irreverent MASH-like style, the film's citations of George Bush generally seem barely straight-faced. To further skewer the geopolitical confusion, the film is festooned with evocations of consumerism, media and popular culture - underlining the increasingly amorphous nature of global affairs (a theme summed up in the exchange between Wahlberg and his torturer - where it's always so clear how easily the roles of captor and prisoner could have been reversed). With the generally well-judged deadpan performances, the audacious mix of styles and moods and the superb physical handling, and the strange desaturated look which makes the movie (aptly) seem like strange science-fiction at times, it's a great achievement - a mainstream movie (the ultimate theme of moral awareness is not of course that uncommon) with a fresh, bull-headed sensibility. Minor reservations: the film seems a bit overlong, and some of the pop culture oriented dialogue (like the talk about Michael Jackson at the start of Wahlberg's interrogation) is getting stale now.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>1999-10-05</date>
		<username>Dan Grant (dan.grant@bell.ca)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>People are people ( exposing the truth about policy and politics )</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I remember when the Gulf War broke out. I remember being naive back then about war and politics. This was before the movie JFK came out and before one of my best friends at the time told me to wake up and open my eyes. It was then that I started to investigate official versions of wars and political fiascos. And it was then that I realized that there are always three sides to everything; the winners, the losers and then the truth. The truth is hardly ever revealed and it is left up to us to fill in the pieces and develop our own theories. We'll probably never really know what happened to JFK. It's probably somewhere in between the Warren report and what Jim Garrison believes. World War II? There are so many stories out there about official this and official that that no one really knows where to look anymore. Take any conflict from Tianemen Square to Watergate or in my country things like the Meech Lake Accord to the fishing dispute that we'll always have with the U.S. There will always be a shroud of mystery to any conflict. Because we aren't supposed to know. But why? That is a question that I don't have an answer to. All I know is that the government thinks that it's better if the public is given limited information on everything so that we stay safe, comfortable and free. But what does all that mean? Because if the truth is out there, I'd like to know it, just like I'm sure the rest of you would too. Three Kings feels the same way and tries earnestly to shed some light on a few issues that certain people think that we would be better off not knowing about. This is one of the best movies of the year and it should be remembered at Oscar time. More on that in a minute.Three Kings is the story of four soldiers that discover there is gold to be hijacked from Saddam who hijacked it from the Kuwaiti's. In the group are four very different personalities. George Clooney is the take charge guy that takes no crap from anyone. Ice Cube is a devout religious guy but he too is determined to live by his own rules. Spike Jones plays a guy that is somewhat of a redneck. He doesn't really understand what his role in the war is. He really thinks that it is cool to kill with extreme prejudice. He doesn't know that his job is liberation, he thinks that they are there to kill every Arab that they can, not even taking into consideration that they are being helped by many Arabs. Mark Wahlberg plays a family man that tries not to have a conscience about what he is doing but he can't help it. When they embark on their journey they are met with ample resistance from not only the Iraqi army but from their own military that doesn't want to stop them from getting the gold ( they have no idea that they are going for the gold ) but they want to prevent them from disobeying conduct that could jeopardize their reputation back home with the American people. Everything is kept hush hush and the media is only allowed to report what they are told they can report. They can't tell the public about the real dangers or what is really happening, if they did, then Bush may look bad in front of his own people, so they are given fallacies and fairy tales to report. That is one of the interesting sub plots to the film.What really stands out is the theme of unity. Is an American life worth more than an Iraqi, Kuwaiti or Iranian life? Are we really that sad of a people that we put our material needs above the welfare of a human being? And as easy as those questions may seem to answer, they really aren't. Not to the person that is honest with themself. If you could get away with about 50 million in gold but knew that people were going to be slaughtered, would you stay and help them or would you go and live with your conscience? Really, what you do? Three Kings asks tough questions and doesn't always give the "right" answer. Three Kings is unflinching when it asks these questions and it doesn't duck anything. It comes right out and tells us that perhaps we are not as noble and benevolent as we think we are.Three Kings is one picture that deserves to receive a slew of Oscar nominations. I think that so far this is best picture material along with best director, supporting actor in Jones and especially in the cinematography department. The shady and hazy look to the film works to perfection as you sense the confusion and feel the ambiance and the heat around you. Having the camera look as though it has sand in the lense was a stroke of perfection. Filming this in perfect wide screen 35mm film would take away from what it wanted us to feel. After all, this isn't a feel good fantasy like Star Wars.Three Kings has so much to say and it is a film that should be seen by everyone. American Beauty is still the best film of the year, but this should be mentioned in the same sentence and it should be remembered in March.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>lou-50</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Politically Correct War Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not many films about the Persian Gulf War have come out. "Courage Under Fire" is the best one. "The Three Kings" is not worth your time. A politically correct film about Iraqis and the Persian Gulf War neatly bundled with enough exploding violence and sidetracked stories to make it seem interesting. "Three Kings" doesn't work as anything but a ?kindlier and gentler' war story. The film tries to present a moral tale about rag-tag men who can somehow, even in the face of war, change and be better. "The Three Godfathers" is a classic moral tale told in a much more direct and realistic way. "Three Kings" gives us superficial characters, stereotypic Arab behavior, and just enough overhanging guilt to paper over a contrived ending. Yes, the coalition forces won the Gulf War but betrayed the embattled rebels in their own futile war with Saddam. George Bush became an overnight savior but his name soon personified the fickle American Army whose presence was felt more in damage and destruction than in liberation. And so in "Three Kings", we have a reversal of form in which four men, whose initial goal is to steal back Kuwaiti gold for themselves, end up doing good instead. The film also highlights insipid soldiers, to the point of making us wonder if they are the only kind of foot soldiers we have. The character, Conrad Vig, is stupid, racist, loud-mouth, and out of control. Equally dumb are the chaperoned soldiers who take an arrogant and aggressive newswoman named Cruz, all over the desert. When the movie finally tries to make a statement about war, it does so badly. Take the interrogation between Troy Barlow and his Iraqi captor. In between electric shocks and physical torture (which don't seem to have any purpose), Barlow empathizes about how he would not want a foreign country to bomb his home and kill his children. "Three Kings" could have been a fair action picture if you take out any messages. Instead, by trying to portray sides equally, it badly diluted any messages at all.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Don't believe the hype!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What can I say about a film that features a cow being blown to bits and played up as a joke? Nothing much positive, unfortunately. Barlow, Gates, Elgin and Vig are Gulf War soldiers who take advantage of the jubilation and confusion at the end of that conflict to steal gold bullion originally stolen by Saddam Hussein's men. The story includes thoughtful commentary on the politics of war (even criticizing the USA's involvement), racism and the human cost of conflict. It mixed satire, political drama, and action, but it felt as if the movie didn't know what it wanted to be. The camera never stops moving, and I found the constant zipping and zagging to be headache-inducing. Very little information is given about the character's backgrounds, other than Barlow (Mark Wahlberg), who's the only family man among the soldiers. He was the only character who changed the most at the end. Vig (Spike Jones), is a Southerner who's not much more than a stereotype. I could tell that George Clooney is constantly improving as an actor, proving he's not just another handsome face. Hopefully, this movie will also finally prove that Ice Cube is more than just a rapper. Even though I understand that a war movie would have bloodshed, much of it is senseless and it is non-stop. There are also incidents of torture, and several scenes have people being riddled with bullets. At one point, the audience is shown what really happens when a bullet enters the body. Fortunately, I have a strong stomach; others might find themselves looking for the exits. A female reporter (good job by Nora Dunn, formerly of tv's Saturday Night Live) is one of the best characters in the film. She's set up as an outsider looking in on the mayhem. She has several funny moments. Three Kings is definitely not for kids or teens (who may be drawn because of the presence of rapper Ice Cube).</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Kings Deserves to Be a Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Three Kings is clearly one of the most original, fresh, and provocative films of this year. First, there was Rushmore, The Matrix, Southpark, The Sixth Sense, The Iron Giant, and now Three Kings. Although not nearly as great, Three Kings is thoroughly set in the vein of Great, absurdist warfare movies like M.A.S.H. and Apocalypse Now. Had I been someone who solely relied on movie trailers to encourage or discourage me from seeing a picture, I would have regrettably missed out on this exciting film. For me, George Clooney has yet to be a successful leading man at the box office, and Ice Cube . well, Ice Cube is Ice Cube. So, a movie having those two in it wouldn't exactly make me salivate at the chance to part with my time and money to go see it. In fact, before seeing Three Kings, I had more respect for the talents of Mark Wahlberg than either of those two. Nonetheless, my doubts about the film were for naught!Three Kings is so multi-faceted that it cannot be pigeonholed into a 60-second vision byte. Yes, there is action as the movie trailer overemphasizes, but if that is all you're interested in, then this film is not for you. The action in Kings is not for its own sake, but is merely another element in the story, just like dialogue and visuals. Too many times, persons who make movie trailers cater to the lowest common denominator - as though no one the audience has ever seen a truck blow up before. How misleading! Instead, Three Kings is hilarious; action-packed; thought-provoking; cynical; balletically violent; at times, beautifully sorrowful; bitingly sharp in its commentary on American consumerism and materialism, and mercilessly critical of the Bush administration's Gulf War policies. It's the best film I've seen since The Sixth Sense. Only, Three Kings is wholly more apt, and deservedly so, at becoming a classic.Rating: 7.5 to 8/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Corrective</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A welcome big-studio remedy to our post-Gulf War sugar high, THREE KINGS seems in its first two reels to be Whit Stillman's LAST DAYS OF BAGHDAD. The writer-director David O. Russell (of SPANKING THE MONKEY and FLIRTING WITH DISASTER) has a Stillman-like bemused-preppie sensibility that just can't work itself up to the gallows humor of MASH or APOCALYPSE NOW. But once the action-movie plot gets in gear, the movie is a fount of invention and of startling truth-telling--a sequence in which an Iraqi torturer recounts the smart-bomb destruction of his family may be the most seditious scene in a studio movie since JFK. The movie is plenty uneven, but it's brave and alive in a way studio movies aren't these days--you get one or two of these a year if you're lucky, so the quibbles don't really matter. And as the gradually wised-up, cornfed reservist hero, Mark Wahlberg has arrived at bona-fide movie-star status--he's the current reigning champ at portraying the confused and agitated decency of Joe Everyman.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fantastic War Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Three Kings" ranks as one of the best war movies of this decade and certainly one of the better films of this year (1999). Its story starts out sort of satirical though it quickly becomes something more meaningful. While it seems to be only about four soldiers trying to get rich off of stolen gold it definitely has a more poignant and deeper purpose. The portrayal of enemy soldiers as actual people rather than stereotypical "bad guys" particularly struck a chord with me, and since its message was one of anti-war (as most war movies are) this aspect was very well done. While it may lack the sheer intensity of something like "Saving Private Ryan" or the thoughtful introspective nature of "The Thin Red Line," "Three Kings" stands on its own by using humor to contrast against the realism seen in the characters. Also of note are the great performances, especially by George Clooney who finally proves that he is a very talented and capable actor. Highly recommended, 4/4 stars.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-10-03</date>
		<username>boomer-40</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Gulf War Opera-Lite</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Most of the time, Hollywood gets it wrong about war. Rambo seems to be the best known "individual" that came out of the Vietnam War, but as someone who experienced that conflict first hand, I don't recall anyone who reminded me of such a person, not even the Green Berets in the fire base I was at. But once in a while Hollywood seems to get it right. "Apocalypse Now" was the "opera" about the Vietnam War. "Grand Illusion" and "Paths of Glory" might be the "operas" about World War I. "Seven Samurai" might be the "opera" about mercenaries. "Three Kings," unless a better one comes along, is the "opera," however offbeat, about the Gulf War. All the elements were there. Frustration about the missed "glory of war," as if there were such a thing. The media, represented in all its self-promoted importance (along with a tiny bit of usefulness). The politics of the war was there as well, showing the audience exactly what it was all about-oil (and to a lesser extent, compact discs). Touching the bases of what has gone before (Catch-22, MASH, even "The Boys in Company C," a Vietnam movie that was far closer to the truth about Vietnam than it has been credited), "Three Kings" was a joy (of sorts) to watch. I saw it on opening day, and went back the next day to see it again. I don't think any comments about the director, actors, script, or the style of cinematography are necessary, because to me this was a film so well made that all that is really unnecessary. To me, I was totally involved in watching the screen and I never really took notice of any of it. Which is how it should be.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Wierd, but worth it</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To quote my friend who I dragged along with me to see this: "That was f*%&amp;ed up."And really, it was. Reason #1: they made a war movie out of the Gulf War. Reason #2: they tried to take that and make it super-dramatic and lighthearted at the same time. Reason #3: it worked.It is funny. The jokes are funny, and there are some things that are so dang wacky, you have to laugh (e.g. nerf footballs filled with C4). And thank God, George Clooney doesn't take himself too seriously this time.And it is dramatic, too. I'm not going to elaborate, because doing so would probably reveal more than I'd like to.There were some things that kinda bothered me... sometimes the way they filmed made things out of focus, and that got to me. I guess it was all part of the plan, but I still didn't like it. And certain parts got pretty over-the top: I felt like Russell was hitting me with a two-by-four saying "THINK THIS WAY!!" (It felt quite a bit like English class...)I can't see how people can compare this to Schindler's List or Saving Private Ryan or Platoon. It may contain elements that could be considered similar to those, but the approach is too different to be able to classify it anywhere close to the same league as those other ones.But it's still a good movie, and worth sinking $8 into.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>1999-10-03</date>
		<username>Andrew-106 (ajames@neptune.on.ca)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>No other 1999 movie is playing in this league</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Thomas Pynchon's novel 1972 "Gravity's Rainbow" will never be made into a movie, certainly if Pynchon has anything to say about it. But its spirit is evident throughout "Three Kings". Both take place in the messy anarchy that characterizes an immediate post-war period. Both revel in the technical details of what other books and movies take for granted. But most importantly, both look at every character dispassionately AND compassionately, whether they are torturers, pedophiliacs or Americans (just kidding - I couldn't resist).Three Kings is a movie of perfect details. The brief sex scene at the beginning cunningly symbolizes the relationship between the military and the media during the Gulf War. I'm fairly confident that the writer/director intended the scene to be symbolic and not merely gratuitous, based on the level of intelligence displayed in the script throughout.The salaam-hair salon verbal riffing scene is hilarious. For those who didn't get it the first time, "salaam" is Arabic for "peace". It's an example of how many of the verbal jokes in Three Kings are not in your face (unlike the sight gags - shredded beef anyone?). You have to go in to get them - which is why Altman's MASH has been used as a reference point by reviewers. The dialogue isn't overlapping in the usual Altman way, but it's not very high in the mix.IMHO, Three Kings goes as far as any American movie could ever go in portraying how the rest of the world sees Americans - both as individuals and as cogs of the government/military industrial complex. The heartbreaking scene [mild spoiler alert] where our heros are loading up the truck with gold, with help from Saddam's troops, while the Shi'a Iraqis beg them not to go not only illustrates a truth about the Gulf War, but about how the US usually comes across as being hypocritical in any of it supposedly do-gooding ventures.Unfortunately, no one movie can erase the damage of decades of Arab demonizing in Hollywood movies. But full marks to Three Kings for trying. The (apparently not that obvious) point is that everyone in the world, including East Timor, is a human being worthy of respect.No movie is beyond criticism. Yes, Three Kings is slow in places (perhaps a result of the attention to detail mentioned above), yes the ending is too Hollywood. Although all the actors - especially those playing the Iraquis - were at least adequate and didn't get in the way of the story, just think of all the memorable character actors in Kelly's Heroes to realize how different Three Kings could have been. But not necessarily that much better. We don't need another (Kelly's) Heroes anyways.Overall, Three Kings is so strong and original in so many areas that it towers above every other film I've seen this year. I have this knawing sense that the script is too pointed yet ambiguous for the movie to be successful, which would be a shame. Not to knock Blair Witch, which was an interesting approach to filmmaking, but this a far more important statement. I hope it makes more money, because this sort of thing has to be encouraged. 9 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>ozman</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Don't miss it!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Roger Ebert sums it up, "some kind of weird masterpiece". Certainly it's the strangest "war picture" since MASH. It reveals, in several ways, just how much of what we think of as "the nineties" derives from the national orgasm that Operation Desert Storm provided this country. From national uncertainty, even a little humility as we watched the Soviet Union topple before our economic might in the late eighties, to gloating vanity as "the American Way" seemed to drive all before it. The idea of America as the world's policeman gained new popularity and the puissance of the American military became, once again, the conventional wisdom. It's all there peeking around the edges of this film and, occasionally, taking center stage.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-10-02</date>
		<username>Brad K.</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A fantastic film which blends action, comedy, and drama perfectly.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Three Kings is one of those rare films that combines action, comedy, and drama without losing anything and making itself a great film. George Clooney (Out of Sight, The Peacemaker) stars as a man in the army who along with Mark Wahlberg (Boogie Nights, The Big Hit), Ice Cube (Anaconda, The Player's Club), and Spike Jonze (director of Being John Malkovich) have come upon a map which they believe leads them to a fortune in stolen gold. The Gulf War is over so the men decide to go after the gold. They find it, but are convinced to stay and help out some locals who are being terrorized by Sadaam's army. The film starts off with comedy, then adds action, then the drama without losing anything it had before and still showing the horrors of war (though there are no major battles or anything). Clooney gives a strong lead performance. Ice Cube and Jonze are both solid. The film's best performance belongs to Wahlberg. Jamie Kennedy (Scream) adds some very funny moments. Highly recommended.</fullReview>

		<username>Ryan Novak (slaw09@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Different</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After seeing the commercials/trailers for this film and hearing the buzz (rumors of Oscar talk) , this movie had become a must see. I wasn't sure where the Oscar talk fit in to a film that looked like a buddy action flick set during the Gulf War. However, the cast does a wonderful job. Spike Jones was wrong, he does deserve to be among this cast. He steals the show in most scenes and is extremely entertaining. The comedy factor in this movie is high but so is the creativity and drama. Anyone expecting nonstop action, well wait for Universal Soldier 3. This movie touches on an element that is left out of most pictures made today and that is the human element . Each character is, well, human. They don't think alike, they don't act alike, they don't act as one. The movie makes use of some interesting "perspective shots" which sometimes amaze and at the same time repulse the crowd (that is what it is used for, so it isn't a bad thing). David Russel and company did a wonderful job on not only making this movie believeable but kept it entertaining. That is what movies are all about.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-10-02</date>
		<username>max winkler-wang (maxwin)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>empathy for enemies</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The most striking aspect of how "Three Kings" presents its story, is its humanization of the Iraqis, the enemies of the Gulf War.One scene involving the torture of an American POW by an Iraqi Republican Guard, speaks volumes in its presentation of the motives of the young Iraqi. He was trained in torture by the Americans, against the Iranians, and speaks in American slang. He is overwhelmed by the crippling of his wife and death of his son in the American bombing of Iraq, and his passion is to make the American prisoner understand his pain and the basis of what he sees the War to be really about. You get that this is not a film about the glories of war, but rather about the pain and chaos. That it is presented in dark humor and comedy gives you the real sense of humanity of the participants. I would rate this movie as between very good and excellent.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>1999-10-02</date>
		<username>bopdog</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Great stuff, good movie, reduced slightly by sophomoric ideology</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is a lot of moralizing in this movie. This is OK, but it can also be dangerous, because it is human nature to confuse our own opinions with fundamental human conditions. Not that we would ever do that, of course, but the movie makers here seem to have a blend of genuine "reality" as well as the fashionable story lines of the ideogogue (that's a fancy term for a "manure" filled person who doesn't recognize the manure-ness of their, and all, opinions).Much of the drama has to do with the horrible situation of a real individual human being getting caught up and included in a big, impersonal event. Here the reduction of a human life (including the families and friends not in the military) to a mere cog, a mere member of a group, a "side" in a conflict, is powerful and moving. Yet these stories were also told as they bridged two real cultures-- Saddam's Iraq on one side and the West on the other. Thus, the writers/director had to select some real features of the times and the politics to portray as backdrop for the movie's story. Precisely here is where they stumbled a bit-- all military authority and hierarchy was depicted as fumbling and inefficient-- with a hint of some hidden agenda, some unnamed evil. The explanation that they war was about "oil rights" was also trotted out as the prime cause of the actual Iraqi war. While I agree that the fabled "liberating" of Kuwait was also bogus as an explanation-- I would guess that most folks saw through that story by the time the real war was over-- the "blood for oil" explanation dished up by the knee-jerk marxists at the time, and since, was equally insulting.There is undoubtedly a grain of truth in these explanations, but they are too shaky and too distracting from other important issues to be worthy of a film maker's or audience's time. Some analysts, including some at the Kremlin, reported later that the primary reason most of the Russian generals refused to support the military coup that happened right after the Iraqi war, was because they had been profoundly demoralized by the swift defeat of their "client" military regime-- Saddam's army and air force. It has been said that the Iraqi war was the proxy for the long dreaded stand-off between the U.S. and the U.S.S.R. Maybe it prevented World Ware III? It was shown that all the Russian military stuff was crap; and the best and bright Soviet advice failed to produce a useful strategy for the Iraqis.Whatever-- "Three Kings" is a great movie because of the harsh edge with which they depict battle. It is also a lame and mediocre movie (in parts), because of the superficial and "media friendly" arguments it sometimes used to criticize the war. It sounded, at times, like a group of 19 year old college students mouthing back the conventional, modern, politically correct versions of why government (read: mom and dad?) is wrong. Come on-- do your own thinking, folks. Learn to see beyond the cliches and slogans (the ideology) that you have been spoon fed. If you want to criticize something-- great! But do it intelligently, with at least some grasp of objectivity, and for the right reasons.Overall-- heavy themes, good job. George Clooney is SO great as an actor-- he really gets to shine here. But all that is tempered and reduced slightly by the corny and unconvincing whining about "The Man," and how he's out to get us all. I gave it an "8."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>1999-10-02</date>
		<username>mattguy</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>absurd war = dark humor</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If you like your humor on the cynical and darkened side then this film will appeal to your sarcastic nature. I saw the film in a city that practically had a wargasm back in '91 with flags, ribbons, and Lee Greenwood saturating every pore of the populace. Laughably, a few in the theatre were visibly upset when the movie began deftly picking apart the ridiculousness of the whole desert storm situation. Yes, to some this film is a skewed patriotic representation of what they might imagine the whole war was about, but I favor the opinions of the film. I'm familiar with reservist who took a couple of months sabbatical to the desert, and the thought of a few of the guys I know with semi-automatic weapons IS an absurd notion. Glorify the "war" if you wanna, but there's enough absurdity to that event to justify and warrant more than a little dark humor. If your sensibilities can't handle that notion and you'd rather sing "Proud to be an American" every Thursday night at the kareokee bar, then stay home and rent Saving Private Ryan...and leave the better movie of 3 Kings to those of us who know how to laugh at irony, tragedy, manipulation, illogicality, and more than a handful of the baser human conditions.This movies highlights those sensibilities in glaring relief to the popular notion of the war. And it does it a fashion that's both subtle AND (yes) heavy-handed. It's not a perfect film, but the moments (and there are more hits than misses) that capture the true essence of war are sublime. Violence is brutal. This film shows it to us in hyper realism; not as a gag either (as some here would have you believe) but as an exercise in authenticity. If you've seen Catch-22, then you know what angle the lens is pointing. If you're favorite movie is The Green Berets or something with Audie Murphy, then stay away from this film (and buy a clue while you're at it).Four outta Five stars</fullReview>
	</review>

And it does it a fashion that's both subtle AND (yes) heavy-handed. It's not a perfect film, but the moments (and there are more hits than misses) that capture the true essence of war are sublime. Violence is brutal. This film shows it to us in hyper realism; not as a gag either (as some here would have you believe) but as an exercise in authenticity. If you've seen Catch-22, then you know what angle the lens is pointing. If you're favorite movie is The Green Berets or something with Audie Murphy, then stay away from this film (and buy a clue while you're at it).Four outta Five stars</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Clooney finally found his breakthrough film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Three Kings: at the end of the Gulf war, four grunts set out to "liberate" the gold Saddam stole from Kuwait but damned if real life doesn't get in the way. This is not your typical war movie i.e. it is not a rah rah recruitment film. The violence is sparse, but when it is onscreen it feels real - no "Oh, don't worry it's just a flesh wound" drivel. The slow motion scenes of bullets meeting bodies, although not as visceral as the opening scenes in Saving Private Ryan, are very effective. In addition, The grainy whiteout quality of the film helps to emphasize the harsh brutality of the landscape, and provides an appropriate backdrop for the ever-present black humor (where else other than an Ozzy Osbourne concert will you get to see exploding quadrupeds?). What really makes this movie work is the camaraderie among the actors.Spike Jonez is hilarious as a moronic hick whose idea of fun is wiring footballs with C-4 and using stuffed animals for target practice. Once again Mark Wahlberg demonstrates that he is a much better actor than a singer (and he just keeps getting better), while Ice Cube does an admirable job in his first time out. George Clooney however has the most to gain: he has finally found a role that capitalizes on his monotone, expressionless style (is he smiling, gritting his teeth in anger, or totally disinterested?). Don't get me wrong, I've liked Clooney since I saw him in "Return of the Killer Tomatoes", and I think this is going to be the movie that makes him bankable, but he doesn't really have a huge acting range. I'll be going back for seconds...</fullReview>

		<username>Jason M. Rothman (jasonrothman@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A powerful, funny, original film meant to rattle the public's perceptions of the Gulf War.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>At the dawn of this decade, as the U.S. was trouncing Iraq, few would have guessed that Operation Desert Storm would inspire any great war movies. After all, the battle was easily won -- and audiences wouldn't pay to see what they'd already seen on CNN, right? But as the decade comes to a close, along comes "Three Kings", a powerful, funny, original -- and, indeed great -- war film meant to rattle the public's perceptions of the Gulf War. The movie is a framework for director David O. Russell's scathing indictment of the Bush administration's hypocritical, self-serving foreign policy. It lobbies heavy criticism at a nation that acts as global policeman -- only when it suits our needs.Some of the imagery comes off as cartoonish, and as Russell tries to drive home his point, the film is at times heavy handed. Shots of an American soldier having crude oil poured down his throat, and a baby's crib being crushed by an American bomb are a bit over-the-top. But Russell is simply trying to combat Gulf War propaganda with some brilliant propaganda of his own.Even bolder is how Russell seemlessly blends his serious complex political statement with moments dark humor, searing drama, and some rollicking Rambo-style action sequences. It's an outstanding piece of work.</fullReview>
	</review>

This movie is more like Schindler's List than Rambo III or even saving private ryan. It presents some morality issues that many Americans probably never thought too hard about. The war is about money, the soldiers get themsleves into trouble over gaining money, what happens to people when they don't have money, and what is money really worth. All these are addressed in the film.I saw this at a preview on the berkeley campus and I'll be sure to see it a few more times.This is a great movie. It isn't a war movie. You don't know when why you're one the verge of crying even though you can't breathe cause you just coughed a lung up from laughing so hard. The film has four great actors and a great director. The only sad this about this movie is that it will never win any big awards because of it's unconventionality.See this movie.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Director and screen writer David O. Russell ("Spanking the Monkey" &amp; "Flirting with Disaster") has fashioned an original, surrealistic and cultural-bender film about the last days of the Gulf war in 1991. Cast is excellent with even the secondary characters having substance and impact.Story revolves around George Clooney leading three soldiers (Mark Wahlberg, Ice Cube and Spike Jonze) in stealing a fortune in gold bars, that Saddam's army has looted from Kuwait. But the mission takes bizarre twists with the Americans having to decide to help civilians or leave with the gold.Although there are hints of "Apocalypse Now" in this film, "Kings" has a decidedly humanistic slant in the midst of battle horrors. Film is realistic in its combat sequences, but the film makers take the easy way out for an ending.Film is anti George Bush with the US military leadership often portrayed as stupid. While the individual soldiers are shown as courageous, doing their job and sympathetic toward civilians.Movie has some memorable vignettes and scenes. One scene, where Clooney is invoking the name of George Bush to get something done, is worth the price of admission. Writing is hip and funny with two hilarious lines about the Dallas metroplex for those of you living there.Besides the four major actors, two others deserve notice. Nore Dunn, as an aggressive cable news reporter, and Jamie Kennedy, as her military escort, steal scenes throughout the movie. In a year of mediocre Hollywood film making about teenagers and supernatural mumbo jumbo, "Three Kings" is a crown jewel to be relished for being different. 9/10</fullReview>

		<username>Patrick Brogan</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A memorable film!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>People think that a picture with George Clooney, Mark Wahlberg, and Ice Cube can not be memorable, or a classic. Well, I have to beg to differ. First if you look at the three actor's and their film career's, you would understand. Ice Cube was in BOYZ N' THE HOOD, Mark Wahlberg was in BOOGIE NIGHTS, and George Clooney was in OUT OF SIGHT. Now all three of these underrated and talented actors star in the new David (FLIRTING WITH DISASTER) O. Russell film titled THREE KINGS. THREE KINGS is set in 1991, as the U.S. troops are celebrating the cease fire from Iraq in Operation Desert Storm. Sgt. Troy Barlow (Mark Wahlberg) and his friend/protege, Pvt. Conrad Vig (Spike Jonze) find a map that leds to a Iraqi bunker with items seized by Sadam Hussein's troops. One of the items is tons of seized Kuwait gold bullion. Meanwhile, Special Forces Captain Archie Gates, get wind of these Iraqi bunkers with gold, and finds out that Sgt. Barlow found a map leading to the bunkers. Gates orders Barlow, Vig, and the religious and serious Sgt. Chief Elgin (Ice Cube) to go with him to steal back the Kuwait gold, and to split it among each other. Gates figures that it's only fair to get this gold since all he did during the operation is baby-sit pouty network journalist Adriana Cruz (Nora Dunn). The soldiers expect this "mission" to be quick in and out job, and that they'll be back before the end of the morning. What the troops didn't expect is the treatment of the Iraq civilians given by Hussein's troops. The civilians are beaten and even killed by Hussein's soldiers, and they ban any shipments of food or water to the people. When the U.S. soldiers see this, they're not suppose to react, since they are to follow President Bush orders on not to interfer with Iraq's politics. But these men begin to have a change of heart, and decide to alter their plans. I was very surprised on how excellent THREE KINGS was. I figured that it would have been another clever action film like FACE/OFF and MATRIX. I was wrong, this film is more than a action film, it has comedy, wit, and even emotional scenes. This film has quite a few disturbing inages of the violence of war, and the terror that many Iraq civilians, including children, were facing. Russell does a unique job of showing images of the character's thoughts and flashbacks. I haven't seen such rapid, yet memorable editing of images since the late Stanley Kubrick's work in A CLOCKWORK ORANGE. Also, Russell does a great homage to Sam Peckinpah with slow-motion gun shots and deaths, similar to the WILD BUNCH. I was pleased with the acting also, Clooney stood out as the tough and intelligent leader, while Wahlberg was also good as the sensitive soldier. And Cube is good as the very serious soldier who has faith in his mission. The plot, also written by Russell, is very witty and great. It mixws a terrific blend of action, comedy, suspense, and drama, to where it's taken seriously and not sarcastic. Before seeing this film, I heard that this film, along with AMERICAN BEAUTY, was a strong Oscar consideration for this year. I didn't believe it, and couldn't believe it, until I saw the movie. Now I'll be very upset if it doesn't get nominated for Best Picture. It does to Operation Desert Storm, what SAVING PRIVATE RYAN did for World War II. This is another war classic, and so far, my favorite film of the year!!! ***** (out of five)</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Clooney's heroes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The 1970 movie 'Kelly's Heroes', staring Clint Eastwood and Donald Sutherland, told the story of a bunch of American soldiers attempting to help themselves to some Nazi gold. The film was fun, amoral (no discussions of the ethics of Nazism or war here) and carried a distinct whiff of the counterculture. Thirty years later, David O. Russell's 'Three Kings' begins in a very similar vein, albeit updated to a setting in the aftermath of the first Gulf war. And the start is similarly fun, although some scenes (for example, those showing the interior of the human body) smack of a young director trying a little too hard to show off. It's the second part of the film that is more problematic: the film tries to develop a heart and a conscience, but the Americans are unlikeable (until they undergo the standard Hollywood transformation into conventional good guys) and the Iraqi characters rather unconvincing. War is ugly, and "we" are not always in the right, but these are rather tame messages from a supposedly scabrous satire; without any guiding political message, the movie can't really say anything of interest when it confronts larger themes. Overall, it left me a little disappointed; you need a viewpoint of your own from which to deconstruct those of others.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2005-08-25</date>
		<username>Jonathon Dabell (barnaby.rudge@hotmail.co.uk)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the most unconventional American movies for a long, long time.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After the original Gulf War, the American forces and their Allies declared the fighting over without closing in for the kill on Saddam Hussein. Instead, the Iraqi citizens were encouraged to rise up against Hussein's regime and topple him themselves, with the promise of American assistance.... but in reality, the Americans failed to provide the assistance they'd promised, and many Iraqis who took up arms against their tyrannical leader were left to face the music. Three Kings is a very unorthodox war film which is set during the truce following the end of the official combat. The story deals with four soldiers who learn that the after-effects of war, and the costs of broken promises, are far greater than they could imagine.Whilst rounding up surrendered enemy fighters, some American soldiers find a map which gives details of the whereabouts of Saddam Hussein's stash of gold bullion. Four soldiers - Major Archie Gates (George Clooney), Sgt Troy Barlow (Mark Wahlberg), Chief Elgin (Ice Cube) and Private Conrad Vig (Spike Jonze) - decide to follow the map with the objective of stealing the gold for themselves. It seems a sound enough plan.... they've just fought a war, a truce has been declared, and they figure they deserve to return home a good deal wealthier for their efforts. However, once into the Iraqi wilderness, they are drawn into the plight of a group of Iraqi freedom fighters who have been encouraged to rise against their dictator. Left to their fate by the unfulfilled promise of American support, these freedom fighters and their families now need desperately to escape to the Iranian border to avoid the terrifying repercussions of their insolence.... and Gates and his men realise that their personal wealth must be put on the back-burner while they help these brave but betrayed Iraqis escape from their hostile homeland.Three Kings is an energetic and sometimes surreal film, with a morally complex heart and a brave enough script to question and even criticise American attitudes towards post-war Iraqi problems. Everything about the film defies conventions; just when you think the film is heading down the Italian Job/Kelly's Heroes route, it turns into something far more challenging and sophisticated. The adventures that befall the quartet of Americans are anything but standard fare (for a great case-in-point, check out the wonderfully outlandish scene wherein Wahlberg makes a phone-call to his wife with an emergency request in the heat of battle!) And the ending manages to strike a note of bitter irony that few Hollywood films of the modern era are courageous enough to provide. The film is disturbing and hilarious; caperish yet totally unorthodox; violent and comical.... a film of substantial intelligence and depth which tackles difficult themes without looking for easy answers.</fullReview>
	</review>

The movie is the exact opposite. Right from the beginning, the flash, high contrast image editing informs you that this story is going to stay in flash and constant motion, that it will believe and never forget it's audience. This movie convicts what all the others isn't doing right with the subject matter, I mean people like Speilberg in his heavy handed war films, I mean James Cameron in "True Lies" or something like that that isn't fair to non-Americans, or if forget about being "fair", in terms of the story, it's just being really, painfully cheap. The joy of it is you cannot put your finger on what will possibly hold them back to make the whole 115 minute movie, after all, the war is over. Yes, they get to use their guns and there's a lot of fuss getting the gold, but I think one of the reasons why it seems so fresh is because you can never picture what the next location will look like. Well, what happens is they get involved in Iraq's political position, they get involved with "necessities" they didn't think they needed, they get involved with their own conscience, yet at the same time, their own greed or dreams. The obstacles weren't so hard to see at all, because you realize the ability of the emotional range. It can take you from the deepest hell of sufferance to the deepest hysterical laugh, all done with exuberantly reinventive and literally penetrating technique. The style was always done with it's own sub-American sort of way, casting people like it does. But what's genuinely memorable is the dialogue, because the script gives every character it's best shot to be true, to be unapologetically seen from all kinds of perspectives that challenges how an audience could feel and think, which at one point or another will win over our trust for the world going on in the screen. From then on, it's no more about explosions or grand finales than it is about the decisions the characters go with, and we won't doubt them. This movie does it all so assuringly and emotionally profound that it transcends the integrity of an audience's anticipations. What more can be said of that? Unforgettable? Magical? What may feel more substantial is some hysterically pleasing moments in the movie. There's too many, for example, the convertible argument, the "day jobs" flashbacks(the flashbacks or out-takes are thrillingly satisfying and lingering), Clooney and the civilians walking while "Stop ou Encore" by Plastic Bertrand is playing cut with an Iraqi head wrapped woman wearing sunglasses, Wahlberg's phone call with his wife, certain music which Carter Burwell also contributed to, the lady news reporter ironically crying or being intimidated by Saddam's army, Spike Jonze playing the love songs in the car on the way to save a kidnapped soldier---touches like that that are reminiscent of "Mash"'s sensibility but more modern and somewhat more at the tip of the edge for today. To tell you the truth, you really don't get so exhilarated like this so easily in movies. It's like what Pauline Kael says: when you realize it doesn't get better than this, it becomes blah blah, I forgot what she said, something depicting nostalgia or personal memory. Of course, you wish George Clooney's character can loosen up a little, since he always seems so wise and confident, though very much convincingly, but you know, the other guys got more of a share for humoring themselves or making themselves vulnerable, which is essentially what acting is about.In the end you almost forget that this movie isn't based on true people or events. David O. Russel, which made his superb comedy "Flirting with disaster" is probably one of last remaining people in Hollywood that still fully believes in its audience and being one of the audience members himself. For such numb and insulting products Hollywood can make these days, given more and more, this is a film that flies above anything of its class and importance, especially for today when we are certainly engaged with Irag in ugliness and the feeling of disbelief for our time. Don't dare to underestimate this movie. Thanks.</fullReview>

		<username>Stroheim-3</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>The Good, The Bad, and The Ugly</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>By watching the trailer and the commercials, one gets the impression that Three Kings is a hybrid of action, comedy, and drama. Well it is - sort of. In reality it is a drama above all with some truly funny lines and scenes thrown in. The action sequences are incredible. Not the kind of incredible that we saw in the D-Day sequence of Saving Private Ryan but more of type that we loved in the Matrix. Don't be fooled by what you see and hear - this movie is a drama.Some excellent points are raised in this film. It is a socially conscious war movie, the likes of which I don't think I have seen. It takes a completely different spin on the Gulf War. It blames the U.S. government for telling the Iraqis to rebel against Saddam and then not helping them as they are slaughtered. Immense sympathy is created for the Iraqi people whether they are civilians or members of Saddam's army. Were we wrong for what we did in 1991? What did we win? Money? Oil? Gold (as the heroes of the film are searching for)? Self respect?Aesthetically, the film is great. The film stock is faded to not only give the film an aged quality but it also shows the washed out view of the war itself. As the heroes become more aware of what is occurring around them and decide to do something, the stock becomes more standard. The color becomes more "normal." Furthermore, the use of hand held cameras and panning gives the viewer a sense of being in the action or cavern or crowd (depending upon the scene). For example, we are cinematically placed inside of a truck as it flips over; on the big screen it looks awesome!For all of the film's great aspects - message, aesthetics, acting (which was great). The writing lacked. Although the characters were well witten, the plot was filled with cliches and became too preachy at times. It is as if sympathy for the Iraqis was being shoved down our throat. The film didn't think that we the viewers would follow what they wanted to say. In addition, there was a great deal of moving around as if the plot wasn't really sure what it wanted to do. What this film lacked, other war films like M*A*S*H excelled in. In all, the script really brought down what could have been a great film.</fullReview>

		<username>Waerdnotte</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Potpourri of style and influence</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Russell has created an interesting hybrid of a movie bringing together the adventure stories of H Rider Haggard, the westerns of Ford / Wayne, and the anti war comedy style of M.A.S.H. Gritty and funny in equal measure, Russell has attempted a biting satire of the war / adventure movie and generally succeeds. However, the need to appeal to a wider audience means the introduction of a few clunky plot devices, and the inevitable happy ending. The acting swings from the professionalism of Clooney, to the increasingly erratic style of Mr Cube. Spike Jones' turn as Conrad Vig is impressive and Wahlberg manages to be inoffensive as the soldier who eventually sees the a reflection of his own life and family in the traumatised Iraqis. The movie maybe tries too hard to spell out the obvious - war is bad, everybody suffers, America can be good as well as bad, not all foreigners are terrorists. But Russell I suppose manages to tread a fine line between US imperialist action / adventure movie and satire of US military incompetence.All-in-all, Worth giving up a couple of hours for.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-06-12</date>
		<username>Robert J. Maxwell (rmax304823@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>War is Heck.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>George Clooney leads a group of ordinary GIs into Iraq in pursuit of Saddam Hussein's gold bullion in the aftermath of the Gulf War. (Kids, that was 1991. We won.) At first the film looks like "Kelly's Heroes." But that's only because the targeted gold allows for an exploration of some of the internal political dynamics of Iraq, and for some outstanding if commercially handled action scenes as well. The gold is a MacGuffin, just a plot point to keep our guys on the move, in and out of danger.The Iraqi soldiers are mostly presented as stereotypical villains. While the Americans look on, the Iraqi army captures a handful of anti-Saddam rebels. Then they shoot a woman at point blank range through the head while her young husband and little girl look on. The scene isn't handled discreetly. The woman falls to the sand and a pool of blood forms under her while the daughter tears herself loose and runs to the corpse, shrieking, "Mamma, mama!" In a way it's the opposite of how Spielberg handled atrocity in "Schindler's List," where such scenes are presented matter of factly, under the assumption that the mature audience knows how horrible these acts are without being kicked in the pants by a screaming child. But then there's an adolescent quality in much of the direction. Sometimes the action is thrilling and slightly comic, an upended truck sliding to a stop only inches from the land mine the driver is goggling at. There's the expectable slow motion as some of the artifacts explode. And the ambient sound shuts off while we follow the trajectories of a couple of particularly important bullets whizzing through the air in slow motion.I'm not sure whether such obvious tricks belong in this kind of movie or not. The script itself, willy nilly, provides plenty of comic moments without the director's having force fed additional laughs to the viewer. If anything, comedic intent at the wrong time drains incidents of some of their dramatic value.The movie paints the Americans as greedy and unethical, maybe, but fundamentally good, pausing in their quest for pelf long enough to rescue damsels in distress, and some guys as well. And it criticizes American policy in the war's wake. George H. W. Bush encouraged Iraqi rebels and then he didn't help them. In light of current events, one is compelled to wonder whether it was such a bad idea to stay out of it. The movie criticizes American policy by seeming to argue only that we should have gone ahead, taken Bagdhad, booted Saddam out, and installed a democracy. In that order. At this point it's beginning to look less like "Kelly's Heroes" and a little like "Rambo." The story has one of our guys captured by Saddam's forces and tortured in an unspeakable way in an underground bunker. But then the plot deals us the joker we could never have expected was still in the deck. One of the Iraqi interrogators, in a polished performance combining malevolence with an impassive but genuine curiosity, quizzes the American prisoner concerning the morality of the war -- and the answers aren't easy to come up with. We can't respond with easily available schema about "you and your kind." The scene is presented without much subtlety. It's obviously posed. And yet it may be the most important and memorable in the film, lifting it out of the action/comedy genre that laid claim to American screens through much of the 1980s and 1990s. It may be obvious but it may be necessary too. Its very starkness may be required to penetrate the firewall we've constructed around our conception of the world as one of Biblical good and evil.Clooney's a good actor, along the lines of Clark Gable. Masculine enough not to be off-putting to men, while (I guess) handsome enough to appeal to women. Interesting enough to have a beer and an argument with.Recommended? Yes. It ought to satisfy anyone whose movie values encompass more than video games.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>RobT-2</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Overrated, but still worth watching...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>...because the good parts of "Three Kings" are so good they keep you sitting through the, well, not the bad parts because there are no really bad parts, but the not-so-good parts. The best part is that from time to time we get to see the impact of the Gulf War on the people of Iraq, both the soldiers who were in it for revenge or just for the extra money (as is the case with so many American soldiers) and the people who were sadly led to believe that the US would support them if they rose up against Saddam. We also get a different, more intense (gut-level, if you will) feeling for the effects of gunshot wounds than we usually do in war movies. There are some bravura camera effects, and some wonderful absurd moments (mild spoiler ahead), topped by the scene where one American soldier captured by Saddam's troops is locked in a storeroom with a box of working cell phones, and starts using one to call his wife back in the USA. (Well, I laughed at that one, anyway.)Unfortunately, though the premise is fascinating, it's not worked out in an especially interesting way, apart from the touches mentioned above. The principal characters are hardly developed at all, and their dialogue is mainly functional. It's a credit to the professionalism and screen presence of George Clooney, Mark Wahlberg, Ice Cube, and Nora Dunn, plus the comic timing (but not the Gomer Pyle accent) of Spike Jonze, that they're as watchable and appealing as they are.Similarly, the action sequences seemed pro forma to me, functioning primarily as raw material to excerpt for the previews. Not having seen his previous movies, I don't know how much experience director-writer David O. Russell has with action sequences, but I don't think his heart (or his gut, whatever) was in them as much as the graphically goopy bit illustrating the internal effects of a bullet wound.The really interesting characters are the Iraqis on both sides, especially their connections to the USA (one went to school in Bowling Green, another was trained by American officers during the Iran-Iraq war). I can imagine a much better movie centering on the Iraqi characters, cutting from time to time to the Americans with their plans for heisting Saddam's gold, and having these plot strands come together about midway through the picture. Such a movie might have topped most of the year-end critics' lists, instead of just placing in their top 10's.Of course, it might have also made less money than "Eyes Wide Shut", "Being John Malkovich", or even "Magnolia". As it is, few movies at this level of commerciality possess any convincing viewpoints outside of mainstream Middle American, and fewer dare to get under the skin of American viewers by criticizing American government policy. So give it its due: "Three Kings" isn't a great movie, but it is worth watching. If you don't care for any of the features I've outlined as positive, you probably won't enjoy it. If those features intrigue you, give "Three Kings" a shot. Who knows, you might actually like it better than I do.</fullReview>

		<username>view_and_review</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Three Clowns</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm writing this review with a huge disclaimer: I don't like war movies. They disgust me, they disturb me, they upset me. War movies typically exhibit the worst character in humans, generally it is the worst character in the "enemy" whereas the good guys--even if slightly flawed like this movie--are still saints compared to the bad guys. And the ones caught in between, the civilians, are just canon fodder. Either they are waiting to be slaughtered by their own or waiting to be saved by the magnanimous forces of good.Three Kings is not fully a war movie but it's darn close. I stopped the movie a third of the way through because I couldn't stomach it anymore and it's not like Ice Cube and Mark Wahlberg are worth watching. I heard enough racial epithets and saw enough berating and killing of the "others" to finally cry "Uncle!" I don't know, maybe this movie has a wonderful ending; I'm not sticking around to find out.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-01-26</date>
		<username>insightblr</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A Beautiful story about Iraq (There People, War &amp; Us Military</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An astonishing movie that keeps you off-balance from the first scene. It starts out a wisecracking, cynical action lark about four Gulf War GIs who see a way to steal a fortune. It ends up a sadly resigned indictment of market-driven American foreign policy, wrapped in a sneakily moving story of personal sacrifice and small, compromised victories snatched from cruel anarchy. That actually counts for a happy ending without feeling like a commercial cop out, and the whole thing is often mordantly funny to boot ? what a balancing act! Sgt. Troy Barlow (Mark Wahlberg) and enlisted men Chief (Ice Cube) and Vig (Spike Jonze) come into possession of a map that shows the location of a bunker full of gold. With the help of thoroughly disaffected special-forces operative Major Archie Gates (George Clooney), they decide to make it their going-away present. "Saddam stole it from the sheiks. I have no problem stealing it from him," says Gates by way of clearing up the heist's ethical dimension, pointing out that amid the post-cease-fire pandemonium, no-one will notice or care what they're doing. Naturally, it doesn't go as easily as they anticipate. Surprisingly, they get sucked into helping a group of anti-Hussein Iraqi refugees who know their only hope of post-war survival lies in fleeing the country before the American military pulls out. The GIs are fairly decent guys, in a lazy kind of way, capable of pretending that wrong is right until somehow utter bloody chaos marches them, one baby step at a time, into doing the right thing at the moment when it stands to cost them the most. This is an amazing leap for writer-director David O. Russell, who graduates from sharply observed, small-scale stories about screwed-up families to a thought-provoking dispatch from a profoundly screwed-up world.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-06-04</date>
		<username>go_W_E_S_T</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>excellent, underrated little gem</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have only seen this movie twice but already I have to say that it has forced its way into my list of favorites. I, like many before me who have critiqued this move on IMDB, would agree that this movie was extremely underrarted and overlooked. Now I'll grant that this movie came out the same year as movies like American Beauty, Fight Club, The Matrix, The Sixth Sense, Magnolia and an endless list of other excellent movies (at least, I can definitely vouch for the first three movies) but still, this movie was comparable to those flicks and deserved at least a few Oscar nominations. It also didn't help that it came out not to far after the like of the more overrated (though still very excellent) war-dramas, ie-Saving Private Ryan and Thin Red Line, but still this movie deserved much better marketing and better acclaim. *SPOILERS MAY OCCUR. JUST DON'T SAY I DIDN'T WARN YOU!* To give a brief plot synopsis, the movie is set during March,1991. Of course the Gulf War has just officially ended. The United States has achieved it objective of liberating Kuwait from the control of Iraq, but has signed a cease-fire agreement with the latter, leaving the now liberated Kuwaitis to fend for themselves against Sadaam and his Republican Guard. The main characters of the movie are Troy Barlow (Mark Wahlberg in an excellent and sometimes heartfelt performance) who just recently became a father, Vig. Conrad, Troy's boisterous and somewhat gun-happy best friend (Spike Jonze, whose performance provides some of the more comical moments of the movie), the cautious and more reserved Chief Elgin (the surprisingly good Ice Cube) who initially can't stand Conrad, and the soon-to-retire Major Archie Gates (the very under-rated George Clooney). Our four heroes have stumbled upon a map that could lead to some bunkers full of Kuwaiti bullion (gold, essentially) stolen by Sadaam's soldiers. They subsequently decide to set off on this journey completely driven by their own self-serving interests and grandiose dreams of wealth and material indulgence. They finally get to the village where the gold is stored. They notice that the villagers are being tortured and starved by Sadaam's troops in a bid to force their allegiance. At first the four soldiers ignore the chaos and proceed with "necessity" as usual. "Necessity" suddenly changes when the four Americans witness a brutal murder by one of Sadaam's troops. The four decide to stay behind and help the villagers. What follows is a tale of transformation and sacrifice. Besides the excellent plot, one thing that I really liked about this movie was its skillful and unique (albeit, sometimes uneven) combination of all the action, drama, emotion and intense violence of an excellent war movie with the razor-sharp wit of a wickedly-funny dark comedy. This trait by itself catapults this movies far apart from most of the war movies that have come out recently. This, by itself, should have turned some heads at the Oscars. To add this, the visual techniques used in the movie were perfect (I especially loved visuals used to show what happens with a gunshot wound) and should have been nominated as well. The dialogue and the chemistry between the main charatcers was also played to perfection. Jaime Kennedy is perfect in a supporting role as the goofy and somewhat inept private Walter Wogaman who has to stall an over-ambitious reporter Adriana Cruz (Nora Dunn) from following the four soldiers Beside being an excellent action-comedy/war-drama, this movie was also a very brilliant satire. It had much to say about the selfishness of man, the pushiness of American media and the way we're seen by other countries. In one scene, we hear an Iraqi soldier use Michael Jackson as an example of how Americans make black people feel inferior and to wish that they were white. Even the main plot of the movie could suggest satire. We see these four American soldiers on this selfish quest for gold suddenly turned into a group of people fighting for a cause that is greater and more valuable than the riches that they seek. We also see the example of the reporter who is so driven by her desire to capture that great story that she would willingly risk her life for it. Now as to why the title of the movie is called Three Kings as opposed to Four, well, without giving much away, one of them does die at the end. Despite this tragic part of the movie and some other equal tragic moments, this movie is definitely worth your time and money.</fullReview>
	</review>

Maybe I'm simply not into movies that call on you to watch the adventures of 4 shallow jackass-American Joe Sixpacks out on the desert and then suddenly slap you with a heartbreaking 'plight of the refugees' plot line. "Three Kings" sure couldn't.Many scenes were cute! This is how smart, scrupulous criticism gets lost: insight and taste are sacrificed in favor of desperate snap judgments failing to see through the haze of desperate filmmaking."</fullReview>

PLOT GIVEAWAYS.For instance, he is taken prisoner by the Iraqis and thrown into a bunker that's filled with a ton of stolen merchandise. Surprise, surprise, there's a bag full of cell phones. Surprise, surprise, the very next phone he picks up works. "So he's about to call some number to get in touch with military officials and tell them where he is so they can rush in and rescue him, right?" is what you're thinking. 		<shortDescription>They three kings of orient aren't</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Someone said that there are too many clever camera tricks. I couldn't agree less. When we get a CLEVER use of the camera - like the way it zips around to follow the chain of causation as one soldier after another breaks the cease fire - it comes as a relief after all the confused, arty rubbish that makes up nineteen twentieths of the film. The camerawork is TERRIBLE. I must say it was a good idea to give us that grainy, washed-out look, but that's like saying that it was a good idea to shoot in colour. Cinematography and editing are still surpassingly bad.There's a lot to be said for the script, and there's nothing wrong, as such, with mixing comedy, drama and political comment in this way. The trouble is that one third of the triad is missing. None of the comedy is funny. The opening half hour in particular is just one big hole: a hip, humourless nothing. Things pick up considerably during the genuinely interesting story that follows, and the film could actually have been good if it hadn't been so badly directed. A lot of the "emotional involvement", we feel, though, is no such thing. We see crude oil poured down a man's throat, and we see LOTS of disgusting shots of people's insides - the kind of cheap stuff that works directly on our stomachs, but bypasses our emotion altogether.I can see what some critics were thinking when they mistook this for a great film. A great film may even be buried here somewhere, underneath the rubble, or perhaps it's just the foundation of a great film: it's hard to tell. It's certainly not great as it stands.By the way, does anyone understand the title? Clearly it's a reference to the Christmas carol - only really popular in America - "We Three Kings of Orient Are". One of the main characters is even kind enough to sing the song for us. But this doesn't help much, since neither the lyrics nor (and I'm stretching here) the mood imparted by the tune, have anything to do with the events in the film.</fullReview>
